tool
1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines
assistant
[Read] REDACTED/benchmarks/mega/outputs/runs/20260619_084649_zai-claude_glm-5.2_03_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/03_kimi_linear_decode/baseline.py
tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""
… +126 more lines

[Read] REDACTED/benchmarks/mega/outputs/runs/20260619_084649_zai-claude_glm-5.2_03_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/03_kimi_linear_decode/check.py
tool
1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""
11	from __future__ import annotations
12

11	"""

Now I understand the problem. Let me check the environment and understand the key dimensions. Key dimensions: - hidden = 2304 - KDA heads = 32, head_dim = 128, so H*Dk = 4096 - MLA heads = 32, kv_lora=512, qk_nope=128, qk_rope=64, v_head=128 - MoE: 64 experts (8 active), 1 shared, intermediate 1024 - group size 128 The big matmuls are: - KDA q/k/v/g_proj: 2304 -> 4096 - KDA o_proj: 4096 -> 2304 - MLA q_proj: 2304 -> 32*(128+64) = 32*192 = 6144 - MLA kv_a: 2304 -> 512+64 = 576 - MLA kv_b: 512 -> 32*(128+128) = 32*256 = 8192 - MLA o_proj: 32*128 = 4096 -> 2304 - MoE gate/up: 2304 -> 1024 (per expert, 8 routed + 1 shared) - MoE down: 1024 -> 2304 The critical insight: at batch 1 (M=1), these are all GEMVs. The fused int4 dequant-GEMV is the win. For MLA at long context: kv_b is applied to the full c_kv cache: c_kv (context_len, 512) @ kv_b (512, 8192) = (context_len, 8192). This is a GEMM, not GEMV. So at long context, this dominates. Wait, but the absorb-vs-materialize fork... Let me think. Actually MLA: the smart move is to NOT apply kv_b to the cache, but instead absorb q into the latent space. The standard MLA trick: instead of computing kvb = c_kv @ kv_b (materializing full keys/values which is expensive at long context), you project q down: q_absorb = q_nope @ kv_b[:, :qk_nope reshaped] (512 dim), then attention in latent space, then apply kv_b to the single weighted sum of values. Let me think carefully. MLA decode optimization: - q_nope is [H, qk_nope=128]. kv_b is [512, H*(qk_nope+v_head)]. - The "absorb" trick: compute q_absorb = einsum('hd,ohd->ho', q_nope, kv_b_nope_reshaped) -> [H, 512]. Then scores_nope = c_kv @ q_absorb -> [ctx, H]. This avoids materializing the full k_nope = c_kv @ kv_b_nope (which is [ctx, H, 128]). - For values: after getting p [ctx, H], compute the weighted value in latent space: o_latent = einsum('lh,lh->h', p, c_kv)?? No wait. v = c_kv @ kv_b_v giving [ctx, H, 128]. The absorb: o = p^T @ v = p^T @ (c_kv @ kv_b_v) = (p^T @ c_kv) @ kv_b_v. p^T @ c_kv = [H, ctx] @ [ctx, 512] = [H, 512], then @ kv_b_v [512, H*128] -> [H, 128]. So you compute o_latent = einsum('lh,lc->hc', p, c_kv) [H, 512], then o = o_latent @ kv_b_v_reshaped. So the absorb approach: c_kv (the 512-dim latent cache) is what you stream. At context 16384, c_kv is 16384*512*2 bytes = 16MB. The materialized k_nope+v would be 16384 * 32 * 256 * 2 = 268MB. Huge difference. So absorb is the way for long context. But wait — the reference does NOT absorb; it does `kvb = (st["c_kv"] @ _wbf(kv_b))` which materializes [ctx, 8192]. At ctx=16384, that's 16384*8192*2 = 268MB write + read. Baseline does the same. So absorbing gives a massive win on the MLA layer at long context. But this isn't the int4 GEMV part — kv_b is the same W4A16 weight. The absorb trick uses kv_b in a different way: a [512,512] matmul (q_nope @ kv_b_nope part) which is tiny (decode M=1), then [ctx,512]@[512,H] = [ctx,H] which is the attention score using c_kv directly. Then o_latent @ kv_b_v. So the MLA absorb is a big architectural win independent of int4 dequant. Good. Let me focus. Let me plan the overall strategy: 1. **Fused int4 dequant-GEMV kernel** (Triton or CUDA). This handles M=1 cases: all the KDA/MLA projections, MoE expert matvecs. Weight is [K, N] int4 packed [K//2, N], scales/zeros [K//128, N]. x is [K] (or small batch). Output [N]. 2. **Fused int4 dequant for larger M** (MLA kv_b in non-absorb, or grouped). Actually with absorb, kv_b is used at M=1 (q_absorb) and M=1 (o projection). So kv_b is always GEMV-ish. Good. 3. **MLA absorb** to avoid materializing big KV at long context. 4. **MoE**: 8 routed experts + 1 shared, each does gate/up/down on M=1. So 9 experts * 3 matvecs = 27 GEMVs per FFN, each [2304->1024] or [1024->2304]. These are GEMVs. The MoE is the dominant cost actually. Let me estimate bytes. Per block, MoE: 9 experts. Each expert: gate 2304*1024 + up 2304*1024 + down 1024*2304 = 3 * 2.36M params. At int4 = 0.5 bytes each = 3*2.36M*0.5 = 3.54MB per expert. 9 experts = 31.9MB per block. 4 blocks = 127MB per token. Plus attention proj ~ 4*(2304*4096*2 + ...) etc. At 1.8TB/s, 127MB int4 = 70µs minimum just for MoE weights. The fused kernel needs to hit close to this. The baseline materializes bf16: 127MB*4 = 508MB? No wait — it reads int4 (127MB) + writes bf16 (508MB) + reads bf16 (508MB) ≈ 1.1GB → ~630µs. So ~9x traffic. Fused should be ~9x faster on MoE. The expected speedup is large. Let me check what's available. Triton on SM120 (Blackwell). Let me check the environment. Let me check the harness wrapping — there are wrappers for `uv`, `python`, etc. and a GPU lock. I should use `uv run` to run things. Let me first check the environment.

Write a fused int4 dequant-GEMV Triton kernel for M=1 (and small M). 2. Write a fused int4 dequant-GEMM for the MLA absorb path. 3. Implement MLA absorb. 4. Wire up MoE with fused expert GEMV. 5. Optimize, profile, iterate. Let me think about the Triton kernel design for the fused W4A16 GEMV. For a GEMV: y[n] = sum_k x[k] * dequant(w[k,n]). Weight layout: w_q [K//2, N] uint8, scales [K//128, N], zeros [K//128, N] bf16. Standard Triton W4A16 GEMM split-K approach: each thread block computes a tile of N outputs. For M=1, we load x vector (length K) and accumulate over K in groups. A clean design: one program per group of N columns (BLOCK_N). Loop over K in blocks of BLOCK_K (must be multiple of group=128). Load x block [BLOCK_K], load packed weights [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N], unpack to [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] bf16, load scales/zeros [BLOCK_K//128, BLOCK_N], dequant, matmul. Actually, the standard approach for int4 GEMV in Triton: each program handles BLOCK_N output columns. For the reduction over K, we accumulate partial sums. To get bandwidth efficiency we want each weight byte read exactly once. Let me design a straightforward correct kernel first, then optimize. For M=1 GEMV: - Grid: (N // BLOCK_N,) - Each program: - acc = zeros[BLOCK_N] - for k_start in range(0, K, BLOCK_K): - load x[k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K] (bf16) [BLOCK_K] - load w_q [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N], unpack -> [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] bf16 - load scales/zeros [BLOCK_K//GROUP, BLOCK_N] - w = (unpacked - zeros) * scales - acc += sum over BLOCK_K of x[:,None]*w - store acc Unpack in Triton: w_q is uint8. We load as uint8, then for the two nibbles. Triton handles this with bit ops. Since BLOCK_K elements come from BLOCK_K//2 bytes, we can load the packed bytes and expand. Actually a cleaner packing for Triton: it's easier if the weight is laid out so consecutive K is contiguous. Here w_q[k//2, n]: even k in low nibble, odd k in high nibble. So byte at (k//2, n) holds k_even (low) and k_odd (high). In Triton, load the [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] tile as uint8. To get the [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N]: - lo = wq & 0xF - hi = (wq >> 4) & 0xF - interleave. But Triton doesn't have an easy interleave for arbitrary tiles. Alternative: process pairs. For each byte, it represents two K elements. We can compute contribution of both nibbles together. Since x for the two K's are x[k] and x[k+1], and the byte holds (w_even, w_odd): - contribution = (w_even - z)*s * x[k] + (w_odd - z)*s * x[k+1] Hmm, but z and s are per-group (128), same for both. So for a pair: - deq_even = (lo - z)*s - deq_odd = (hi - z)*s - acc += x_even * deq_even + x_odd * deq_odd But to use Triton's dot product efficiently, we want a [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] bf16 tile and do tl.dot(x[None,:], w). For M=1 dot is just an elementwise*reduce, but tl.dot wants at least 16. We can make x a [1, BLOCK_K] and w [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N], tl.dot -> [1, BLOCK_N]. That works and uses tensor cores. To build the [BLOCK_K, BLOCK_N] tile from packed bytes, I can do the interleaving via tl.where on an index, or load two separate tiles: one for even indices, one for odd. Actually simplest: load packed as [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N], expand lo into a [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] and hi into [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N], dequant each, and combine with x_even, x_odd separately: acc += tl.dot(x_even[None,:], w_even_deq) + tl.dot(x_odd[None,:], w_odd_deq) where x_even = x[0::2], x_odd = x[1::2], each [BLOCK_K//2]. That's clean! Each byte contributes to two separate dots. This avoids interleaving. So per K-block: - x_blk = x[k_start:k_start+BLOCK_K] # [BLOCK_K] - x_even = x_blk reshaped to [BLOCK_K//2] (stride 2)... Triton: load with stride. Easier: just load x as [BLOCK_K//2] with offset 2*k and another with offset 2*k+1. - wq = load [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N] uint8 - lo = (wq & 0xF).to(bf16), hi = (wq>>4).to(bf16) - s,z: load [BLOCK_K//128, BLOCK_N]; need broadcast to [BLOCK_K//2, BLOCK_N]. Each group of 128 K (= 64 pairs) shares a scale. We can use tl.expand or load with broadcast via offsets. Simpler: load scales indexed by group and use the group id broadcast. In Triton, load scales at offsets [k_pair // (GROUP//2)] ... that's expensive. Better: make BLOCK_K = 128 (one group), so the whole tile shares ONE scale/zero per column → s,z are [BLOCK_N] vectors. That's clean and matches group size! If BLOCK_K=128=GROUP, then within each K-block, all 128 elements share the same scale/zero (per column). So: - s = scales[k_start//128 : k_start//128+1, :] -> [BLOCK_N] - z = zeros similarly -> [BLOCK_N] - For the 64 pairs: lo,hi each [64, BLOCK_N] - deq_lo = (lo - z[None,:]) * s[None,:] # broadcast [64,BLOCK_N] - deq_hi = (hi - z) * s - x_even = x[2k], x_odd = x[2k+1] for k in 64 - acc += tl.dot(x_even[None,:], deq_lo) + tl.dot(x_odd[None,:], deq_hi) BLOCK_K=128 means K-loop has K/128 iterations. For K=2304: 18 iterations. For K=4096: 32. Fine. tl.dot with M=1: need to pad. tl.dot requires dims >=16 typically and M-dim >=16 on some hardware. We can use [1,64]@[64,BLOCK_N]. Triton allows M=1? Actually tl.dot min is usually 16x16x16 but Triton 3.x may allow smaller with fallback. Padding wastes compute but for M=1 GEMV it's memory bound anyway, so compute doesn't matter much. Actually for memory-bound GEMV, the tensor core efficiency is irrelevant — what matters is reading weights once. So a simple reduce approach is fine. But tl.dot is convenient. Output y [N] bf16 (or fp32 then convert). ```python @triton.jit def w4_gemv_kernel(x_ptr, wq_ptr, s_ptr, z_ptr, y_ptr, K, N, stride_xk, stride_wqk, stride_wqn, stride_sg, stride_sn, ... , BLOCK_N: tl.constexpr, GROUP: tl.constexpr): pid = tl.program_id(0) # over N blocks cols = pid*BLOCK_N + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_N) # [BLOCK_N] # accumulate acc = tl.zeros([BLOCK_N], dtype=tl.float32) ngroups = K // GROUP # x even/odd: process pairs PAIRS = GROUP // 2 # 64 for g in range(ngroups): k_base = g*GROUP # load x even/odd for this group k_idx = k_base + tl.arange(0, PAIRS)*2 # [64] x_even = tl.load(x_ptr + k_idx*stride_xk) # [64] x_odd = tl.load(x_ptr + (k_idx+1)*stride_xk) # [64] # load packed bytes [64, BLOCK_N] byte_off = (k_base//2 + tl.arange(0,PAIRS))[:,None]*stride_wqk + cols[None,:]*stride_wqn wq = tl.load(wq_ptr + byte_off) # [64, BLOCK_N] uint8 lo = (wq & 0xF).to(tl.float32) hi = ((wq >> 4) & 0xF).to(tl.float32) # scale/zero for this group, per column s = tl.load(s_ptr + g*stride_sg + cols*stride_sn) # [BLOCK_N] z = tl.load(z_ptr + g*stride_sg + cols*stride_sn) # dequant lo = (lo - z[None,:]) * s[None,:] hi = (hi - z[None,:]) * s[None,:] # accumulate acc += tl.sum(x_even[:,None]*lo, axis=0) acc += tl.sum(x_odd[:,None]*hi, axis=0) tl.store(y_ptr + cols, acc.to(tl.bfloat16)) ``` Wait, the dequant formula: w[k,n] = (unpack - zeros[g,n]) * scales[g,n]. Yes. This kernel reads each weight byte once. Good. BLOCK_N tuning: I'll use 128. Each program: BLOCK_N output cols. Grid = N//BLOCK_N. For efficiency, x should be broadcast efficiently. Actually each program loads the full x over the loop (ng * 2 loads of 64). Total x reads per program = K. Across all programs = K * (N/BLOCK_N). x is 16KB, cached in L2 (96MB L2 on Blackwell). Fine. But ckv's c is dim 512, qabs's i is dim 512. einsum contracts the matching... no! einsum contracts only letters that appear in inputs but not output. 'lc,hi->lh': input letters l,c,h,i. output l,h. So c and i are both summed? No — c appears only in first input, i only in second. Both are not in output, so BOTH are contraction dims. But c and i are DIFFERENT axes (different letters)! einsum('lc,hi->lh', A, B) = sum_{c,i} A[l,c] B[h,i]. That's summing over BOTH c and i independently — that's an outer product contraction, giving [l,h] = (sum_c A[l,c]) * (sum_i B[h,i]). That's WRONG! I wanted sum_c A[l,c] B[h,c] — i.e., c and i should be the SAME letter. The bug: I used different letters 'c' and 'i' for what should be the same contraction axis. It should be 'lc,hc->lh' or 'li,hi->lh'. So `sabs = einsum('lc,hi->lh', ckv, qabs)` is wrong. Should be `einsum('lc,hc->lh', ckv, qabs)` since qabs is [h,i] and I want to contract ckv's c (512) with qabs's i (512) — same axis → same letter. Let me use 'li,hi->lh'. THAT's the bug. The einsum letters must match for the contracted dimension. Let me fix in solution.py. In my solution.py MLA: - `qabs = torch.einsum("hd,ihd->hi", q_nope, W_nope)` — 'hd,ihd->hi': contract d, free h,i. ✓ correct (d only in both). Should be 'lc,hc->lh' (c matches i). Fix to "lc,hc->lh". - `u = torch.einsum("lh,li->hi", p, ckv)` — p [l,h], ckv [l,i]. contract l, free h,i. ✓ correct (l in both). - `o = torch.einsum("hi,ihd->hd", u, W_v)` — u [h,i], W_v [i,h,d]. contract i, free h,d. ✓. So only score_nope is buggy. Let me fix it.
assistant

Found it. The bug is in my score_nope einsum: "lc,hi->lh" uses two different letters (c and i) for what should be the same contracted 512-dim axis, so einsum sums them independently instead of doing a dot product. It must be "lc,hc->lh".

For st: the KDA states (S, cq, ck, cv) and MLA cache need to be in static buffers too. - The KDA state S[32,128,128] is fixed size — easy to put in static buffer. cq/ck/cv [3,4096] fixed — easy. - MLA c_kv grows. For the graph to be replayed, ALL inputs must be at fixed addresses. If st changes address each step (because cat creates new tensors), the graph can't use st directly. So I'd maintain my OWN static state buffers, and on step() copy st into them (first time / when reset), run graph, copy out to st. The copy overhead: copying st in/out each step. KDA S = 32*128*128*4(fp32)=2MB ×3 layers=6MB. cq/ck/cv small. MLA c_kv = ctx*512*2 up to 16MB. k_rope small. hidden 4KB. Total copy ~22MB in + 22MB out = 44MB/step → 24µs. Plus the graph runs ~0.3ms. Net ~0.35ms vs current 2.2ms. ~6x → total ~15x. But maintaining the copy is complex and the growing MLA cache: if I copy st's c_kv into my static buffer each step, the static buffer must be ≥ current length, and the attention uses current length. If static buffer is CAP and I copy [0:length] in, then attention over [0:CAP] with mask. But copying length rows where length grows — the copy is dynamic-length. For the graph I'd need fixed-length copy (copy CAP rows always). That means my static c_kv is always [CAP,512] full; each step I copy the caller's [length,512] into static[0:length] and leave [length:CAP] as whatever (masked out). The copy of [length,512] is dynamic — but I can do a fixed CAP copy if static mirrors... no, the source is [length] which changes. Ugh. The growth really is the crux. Alternative: decouple. Don't grow the caller's cache inside the graph. Instead: - The graph computes the new latent c_kv_new and k_rope_new (the per-token projections) — these are fixed size [512], [64]. - The graph computes attention against the CURRENT cache (fixed... no, current cache length varies). The attention length varies per step. There's no way around it: decode attention is against a growing set. Real engines handle this in CUDA graphs by pre-allocating the paged KV cache to max_seq_len and using a mask/seq_lens. The attention kernel reads seq_lens (a runtime value, but the SHAPE is fixed=max). So the kernel always processes max positions but skips/ masks based on seq_len. This IS graph-safe because shape is fixed, only values change. So: pre-allocate c_kv_static [CAP, 512], k_rope_static [CAP, 64]. Maintain seq_len (scalar). Attention kernel reads c_kv_static[0:CAP] (fixed shape) but applies mask[c:CAP]=-inf where mask depends on seq_len. The new token's latent is written at index seq_len (scatter, runtime index but fixed shape [1,512]). seq_len increments. For this to be graph-safe: all ops have fixed shapes. The mask must be computed from seq_len inside the graph. mask[i] = (i < seq_len). That's a fixed-shape op (compare arange(CAP) < seq_len_scalar_tensor). Graph-safe (no value-dependent Python branching). The scatter write c_kv_static[seq_len] = new: index from scalar tensor, fixed shape — graph-safe. seq_len += 1: in-place add on scalar tensor, graph-safe. So the WHOLE step can be graph-captured IF I rewrite MLA to use the pre-allocated paged cache + mask, and KDA states as fixed buffers. And the step() interface copies the caller's h into static_in and returns static_out. For the state: on the first step (or when I detect a new/fresh state via e.g. comparing pointer or a reset flag), I copy the caller's initial cache into static buffers and set seq_len. Subsequent steps just use static buffers (I ignore the caller's growing st except to return something).  # --------------------------------------------------------------------------- #
 # helpers
 # --------------------------------------------------------------------------- #
+@triton.jit
+def _rmsnorm_kernel(x_ptr, w_ptr, o_ptr, N, EPS: tl.constexpr, BLOCK: tl.constexpr):
+    offs = tl.arange(0, BLOCK)
+    mask = offs < N
+    x = tl.load(x_ptr + offs, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
+    var = tl.sum(x * x, axis=0) / N
+    inv = tl.rsqrt(var + EPS)
+    w = tl.load(w_ptr + offs, mask=mask, other=0.0).to(tl.float32)
+    out = (x * inv * w).to(tl.bfloat16)
+    tl.store(o_ptr + offs, out, mask=mask)

+@triton.jit
+def _silu_mul_kernel(g_ptr, u_ptr, o_ptr, D, BD: tl.constexpr):
+    pid_m = tl.program_id(0)
+    pid_d = tl.program_id(1)
+    offs = pid_d * BD + tl.arange(0, BD)
+    base = pid_m * D + offs
+    g = tl.load(g_ptr + base).to(tl.float32)
+    u = tl.load(u_ptr + base).to(tl.float32)
+    r = (g * tl.sigmoid(g)) * u
+    tl.store(o_ptr + base, r.to(tl.bfloat16))

assistant

Done. Final result: 11.03x geomean speedup over the optimized PyTorch baseline (1950 tok/s at ctx=2048, 1725 tok/s at ctx=16384), correctness 0.9999 cosine across all seeds/contexts.

What the solution does

solution.py keeps the exact reference module/buffer names (loads the reference state_dict strict) and beats baseline.py four ways:

  1. Fused int4 dequant-GEMV (Triton, written from scratch). Two kernels — a single GEMV for the attention projections and an expert-batched one for MoE — stream the int4 weights once and fuse the nibble-unpack + per-group asymmetric dequant into a tl.dot (tensor cores), so the bf16 weight is never materialized. Split-K (round-robin, fp32 atomic reduction) supplies batch-1 parallelism; the expert kernel indexes the full weight table directly (no gather copy). Hit ~900-1000 GB/s vs the naive sum-kernel's ~380 GB/s.
  1. MLA weight absorption. q is projected into the 512-d latent and attention runs against the compressed c_kv cache directly, so the [ctx, 8192] KV tensor is never materialized — long context is nearly free (+66µs from ctx 2048→16384).
  1. CUDA Graph capture/replay. Batch-1 decode is dispatch-bound (~90 tiny kernels × ~4µs launch). The whole step is captured into one graph and replayed. To make the step shape-stable I rewrote MLA's cache as a padded buffer + length mask + in-place append (no growing cat), and made all recurrent state update in-place on static buffers.
  1. Fused elementwise kernels. Custom Triton kernels for rmsnorm, the gated-delta recurrence, the short causal conv, MoE silu(g)*u + weighted expert sum, and RoPE. Also fused KDA's q/k/v/g into one batched call and MoE's gate+up into one batched call.

Profiling arc

baseline 5.5-6.7 ms/tok → fused GEMV alone (2.7x) → +CUDA graphs (5.3x, killed dispatch) → +softmax contiguity fix (5.3→...) → +dot-based GEMV (6.5x) → +fused KDA/MoE kernels (10.7x) → +fused RoPE (11.0x). Now GPU-bound at ~0.51-0.58 ms/tok; the remaining time is dominated by fundamental int4 weight streaming.

check.py prints PASS; benchmark.py reports peak_fraction: 11.03 / RESULT: OK. No forbidden libraries used (verified by scanning for every token in problem.yaml).

One correctness note worth flagging: I dequantize the small kv_b projection to bf16 once per step (8 MB, read once) rather than fusing it into a GEMV — the transposed contraction it needs in the absorb path would need a separate repacked layout, and kv_b is tiny next to the MoE/projection traffic. Everything else is fully fused.
